Ce%3A%22%25%22%2Ci%3A%22%5C%5Cn%22%2Cr%3A1%7D%2C%7BcN%3A%22identifier%22%2Cb%3A%22%60%22%2Ce%3A%22%60%22%2Cr%3A0%7D%2C%7BcN%3A%22string%22%2Cb%3A%27%22%27%2Ce%3A%27%22%27%2Cc%3A%5Bhljs%2EBE%5D%2Cr%3A0%7D%2C%7BcN%3A%22string%22%2Cb%3A%22%27%22%2Ce%3A%22%27%22%2Cc%3A%5Bhljs%2EBE%5D%2Cr%3A0%7D%2C%7BcN%3A%22paren%22%2Cb%3A%22%5B%5B%28%7B%5C%5C%5D%29%7D%5D%22%2Ce%3Ahljs%2EIMMEDIATE%5FRE%2Cr%3A0%7D%5D%7D%7D%3B%0Ahljs%2EinitHighlightingOnLoad%28%29%3B%0A%0A"></script>

<div class="container-fluid main-container">


<p>Obama has issued two vetoes, the least number of vetoes of any President in history. Is this relatively few vetoes?</p>

<p>While answering this question, I discovered:</p>

<ul style="list-style-type: decimal">
<strong>
<li>81% of bills introduced into congress since 1973 maintain a state of "referred", indicating they have died in committee.</li>
<li>There is weak evidence to support the claim the number of bills enacted per session is declining.</li>
<li>The congressional sessions during the Reagan presidency experienced 10 times more resolutions than the sessions before and after Reagan.</li>
</strong>
</ul>

<p>Let’s look at the numbers.</p>
<pre><code>## Loading required package: DBI</code></pre>
<pre><code>## Warning: package 'DBI' was built under R version 3.1.1</code></pre>
<pre><code>## Warning: package 'dplyr' was built under R version 3.1.1</code></pre>

## 
##     intersect, setdiff, setequal, union</code></pre>
<pre><code>## Warning: package 'reshape2' was built under R version 3.1.2</code></pre>
<div id="has-congress-been-busy" class="section level2">
<h2>A majority of bills stagnate in committee</h2><hr></hr>
<p>To assess the number of relative vetoes, we will compare the number of vetoes with the number of bills enacted.</p>

<p>There are more than just a few states a bill can be in however: Each bill record maintains a state attributed, initiated as "introduced", and progressing into as many as 29 different states. For a complete listing of these statuses, see <strong>Appendix A: 29 Flavors of Legislative State</strong> at the bottom of this post.</p>

<p>In order to make these states digestable, I collapsed them into 9 states:</p>

<ul style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<li><strong>Introduced:</strong> The bill was introduced.</li>
<li><strong>Referred:</strong> The bill was referred to committee but never reported.</li>
<li><strong>Reported:</strong> The bill was addressed by its committee and reported to Congress.</li>
<li><strong>Enacted:</strong> The bill was enacted. This includes bills enacted by the ten-day rule and bills enacted by “unknown”.</li>
<li><strong>Enacted veto override:</strong> The bill was enacted by Congressional override of a Presidential veto.</li>
<li><strong>Passed bill:</strong> The bill passed Congress but was never enacted before the end of the session.</li>
<li><strong>Congressional stalemate:</strong> The bill was never passed because of one of the following reasons: it did not receive mutual approval in the House and Senate; non-identical forms were passed; it was killed by a suspension vote; it failed vote for cloture or it was killed while resolving differences.</li>
<li><strong>Vetoed:</strong> The bill was vetoed successfully. This includes pocket vetoes.</li>
<li><strong>Resolution passed:</strong> Passed simple and concurrent resolutions.</li>
</ul>
<div id="of-bills-in-us-history-remain-in-a-referred-state" class="section level3">
<h4>81% of bills introduced since 1973 are in the “referred” state.</h4><hr></hr>

<p>In the <a href="https://www.govtrack.us/developers/api">govtrack.us database there are 335,487 bills</a>. While inspecting the govtrack.us database, I was interested to find how many bills stagnate in committee.</p>

<p>If only looking back to 1973 an overwhelming majority, 81%, of bills remain in the referred state. Being referred to committee and then held at that level, as opposed to being sent to subcommittee means one of two things: either the bill will be acted on quickly or it is being suppressed and will die from neglect (<a href="http://www.lexisnexis.com/help/CU/The_Legislative_Process/Stage_3.htm">source: LexisNexis</a>).</p>
<pre><code>## Source: local data frame [9 x 3]
## 
##           current_status.f  total   freq
## 1    enacted_veto_override     30 0.0001
## 2              passed_bill     78 0.0003
## 3                   vetoed    245 0.0009
## 4               introduced   1726 0.0061
## 5                 reported   9819 0.0348
## 6                  enacted  11553 0.0409
## 7 congressional_stalemates  12475 0.0442
## 8        resolution_passed  15703 0.0556
## 9                 referred 230879 0.8172</code></pre>

<h4>The total number of bills enacted by congress is declining.</h4><hr></hr>
<p>By visual inspection, I surmise the number of bills enacted by Congress is declining over time.</p>

<h4>The number of bills introduced is also declining.</h4><hr></hr>
<p>I also surmise the number of bills introduced is also declining, however visual analysis is thwarted by the Reagan sessions towering over their neighbors.</p>

title alt width="672" /></p>

<p>It is impossible to ignore the 97th through 100th congressional sessions. Congressional activity, specifically new resolutions, spiked during Ronald Reagan’s presidency. Comparing the 4 sessions before, during and after the Reagan presidency revealed <strong>nearly 10 times more house resolutions from the Reagan years still exist in the introduced state as before and after the Reagan years.</strong> From those sessions, 996 house resolutions are still in the “introduced” state, whereas before and after Reagan sessions only 103 and 26 house resolutions still exist as “introduced”.</p>

<div id="does-obama-veto-a-lot" class="section level2">
<h2>Obama has issued the least total and relative number of vetoes.</h2><hr></hr>
<p>Obama has been president during the 111th, 112th and 113th Congressional sessions. Both in total and relative numbers, he has vetoed the least bills of any president since 1973.</p>

title alt width="672" /></p>

<div id="appendix-a-29-flavors-of-legislative-state" class="section level3">
<h3>Appendix A: 29 Flavors of Legislative State</h3><hr></hr>
<div id="identity" class="section level4">
<h4>Identity</h4><hr></hr>
<ul style="list-style-type: decimal">
<li><strong>referred:</strong> The bill was introduced and referred to a committee, but never reported.</li>
<li><strong>reported:</strong> The bill was adressed in a hearing by its committee, but was never enacted by Congress.</li>
<li><strong>introduced:</strong> The bill was introduced.</li>
<li><strong>enacted_veto_override:</strong> The bill was enacted after a congressional override of the Presidential veto.</li>
<li><strong>passed_bill:</strong> The bill passed Congress but was never enacted before the end of the session.</li>
</ul>
</div>
<div id="congressional-stalemate" class="section level4">
<h4>Congressional Stalemate</h4><hr></hr>
<ul style="list-style-type: decimal">
<li><strong>pass_over_senate:</strong> The bill passed the Senate but not the House.</li>
<li><strong>pass_over_house:</strong> The bill passed the House but not Senate.</li>
<li><strong>pass_back_house:</strong> The bill passed by both chambers but was passed in non-identical forms and the differences were never resolved.</li>
<li><strong>pass_back_senate:</strong> The bill passed by both chambers but was passed in non-identical forms and the differences were never resolved.</li>
<li><strong>fail_originating_house:</strong> The bill failed in the House.</li>
<li><strong>fail_originating_senate:</strong> The bill failed in the Senate.</li>
<li><strong>conference_passed_house:</strong> The bill was introduced in a previous session of Congress. It was passed by both chambers but it passed in non-identical forms.</li>
<li><strong>fail_second_house:</strong> The bill passed in senate, failed in house.</li>
<li><strong>failed_second_senate:</strong> The bill was passed in the House but failed in the Sentate.</li>
<li><strong>conference_passed_senate:</strong> The bill was introduced in a previous session of Congress. It was passed in non-identical form and only one chamber approved a conference report to resolve the differences.</li>
<li><strong>prov_kill_suspensionfailed:</strong> A catch-all for random ways for a bill to die in Congress. The bill was introduced but was killed due to a failed vote for cloture (placing a time limit on the debate), under a fast-track vote called ‘suspension’ (meant to quickly pass non-controversial bills), or while resolving differences.</li>
<li><strong>prov_kill_cloturefailed:</strong> Same as description above for ‘prov_kill_suspensionfailed’.</li>
</ul>
</div>
<div id="vetoed" class="section level4">
<h4>Vetoed</h4><hr></hr>
<ul style="list-style-type: decimal">
<li><strong>prov_kill_veto:</strong> The bill was vetoed by the President and Congress did not attempt an override.</li>
<li><strong>vetoed_pocket:</strong> The bill was pocket vetoed. A bill is pocket vetoed when the President does not sign the bill and Congress adjourns before the ten-day period expires.</li>
<li><strong>override_pass_over_house:</strong> The bill was vetoed by the President and Congress did not finish an override before the end of the Congressional session.</li>
<li><strong>vetoed_override_fail_second_senate:</strong> The bill was vetoed. The Senate attempted to override the veto but failed.</li>
<li><strong>vetoed_override_fail_originating_house:</strong> The bill was vetoed. The House attempted to override the veto but failed.</li>
<li><strong>vetoed_override_fail_originating_senate:</strong> The bill was vetoed by the President and Senate did not finish an override before the end of the Congressional session.</li>
<li><strong>vetoed_override_fail_second_house:</strong> The bill was vetoed by the President and the House did not finish an override before the end of the Congressional session.</li>
</ul>
</div>
<div id="enacted" class="section level4">
<h4>Enacted</h4><hr></hr>
<ul style="list-style-type: decimal">
<li><strong>enacted_signed:</strong> The bill was enacted after being signed.</li>
<li><strong>enacted_unknown:</strong> The bill was enacted.</li>
<li><strong>enacted_tendayrule:</strong> The bill became enacted after ten days elapsed after being presented to the President.</li>
</ul>
</div>
<div id="passed-resolutions" class="section level4">
<h4>Passed Resolutions</h4><hr></hr>
<ul style="list-style-type: decimal">
<li><strong>passed_concurrentres:</strong> The concurrent resolution was agreed to by both Chambers, which is the end of the legislative process for concurrent resolutions.</li>
<li><strong>passed_simpleres:</strong> The bill is a simple resolution and was passed^.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong><em>simple resolution:</em></strong> Designated “S. Res.,” simple resolutions are used to express nonbinding positions of the Senate or to deal with the Senate’s internal affairs, such as the creation of a special committee. They do not require action by the House of Representatives. (<a href="https://www.senate.gov/reference/glossary_term/simple_resolution.htm">source</a>)</p>
</div>
</div>

</div>
